♠ Original name ♣ Abbasid Caliphate I ♥
♠ Alternative names ♣ Abbasid Caliphate; al-Khilafah al-Abbasiyyah; First Abbasid Caliphate Period ♥ al-Khilāfah al-‘Abbāsīyyah
♠ Peak Date ♣ 809 CE ♥ The end of the reign of Harun- al Rashid (763-809 CE) whose rule is described in The Thousand and One Nights. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_1_IqAbbs1] Or alternatively the year range 786-809 CE, which the whole reign of Harun- al Rashid (763-809 CE) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_2_IqAbbs1].
♠ Duration ♣ 750-946 CE ♥ In 750 CE the Abbasid Dynasty took over power from the Umayyad Dynasty. In 946 CE the Caliph lost autonomy when it was taken over by the Daylamite Buyids, reducing the Caliphate to a nominal figurehead of the Islamic world. This resulted also in the loss of an independent military. The Caliph still retained prestige and could influence legitimacy in its former territory. In 1258 CE Baghdad, the capital, was sacked by the Mongols and this extinguished the last vestigial power of the Abbasid Dynasty, although the Abbasid Caliphate was restored in Cairo in 1261. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_3_IqAbbs1]
♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state; nominal ♥ unitary state: 750-936 CE; nominal: 937-946 CE In 936 CE, the Caliphate lost substantial powers of authority and was reduced in its ability to control outlying territory because of bankruptcy and the disintegration of the army. The Caliph granted Ibn Ra'iq the control of military and civil power. Ten years later, the Daylamite Buyids conquered Baghdad, reducing the Abbasid Caliphs to figureheads. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_4_IqAbbs1].

♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ continuity ♥
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Buyid Confederation ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Perso-Islamic ♥ "Abbasid architecture was influenced by three architectural traditions: Sassanian, Central Asian (Sogdian) and later, during the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, Seljuk."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_5_IqAbbs1] Abbasid military was dominated by the Persian technology and tradition.

♠ Capital ♣ Kufa; Baghdad; Al-Raqqah; Samarra; Baghdad; Merv ♥ {Kufa: 750-762 CE; Baghdad: 762-833 CE; Al-Raqqah: 796-809 CE; Samarra: 833-893 CE; Baghdad 833-946 CE} [MAJIDBENAM_REF_6_IqAbbs1]. Samarra was the military and administrative headquarters of the Caliphate 836-870 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_7_IqAbbs1] Merv was the capital of the caliphate from 810-819 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_8_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Both Baghdad and Samarra were purpose built cities.
♠ Language ♣ Arabic ♥ Regional languages included Aramaic, Armenian, Berber, Coptic, Georgian, Greek, Kurdish, Persian, Oghuz Turkic. However, Arabic became the language of trade and governance in a vast territory.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_9_IqAbbs1]. In Egypt the adoption of Arabic as the language of local government took over 100 years. Initially almost all papyruses were written in Greek. 643 CE saw the first bilingual Greek-Arabic document and 719 the last. Earliest known Arabic only document is dated 709 CE. The last papyrus written in Greek was in 780 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_10_IqAbbs1]♠ Linguistic family ♣ ♥

♠ Alternate Religion Genus ♣ Islam ♥
♠ Alternate Religion Family ♣ Shia ♥ "The Abbasids also found themselves at odds with the Shia Muslims, most of whom had supported their war against the Ummayyads, since the Abbasids and the Shias claimed legitimacy by their familial connection to Muhammad (Pbuh). Once in power, the Abbasids embraced the Sunni faith and disavowed any support for Shia beliefs. That led to numeroud conflicts, culminating into an uprising in Mecca in 786, followed by widespread bloodshed and the flight of many Shias to the Maghrib, where the survivors established the Idrisi Kingdom."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_12_IqAbbs1]
♠ Alternate Religion ♣ ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_13_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In 750 CE, following a revolt, Abbasid rulers took power from the Umayyad Dynasty under Abu al-'Abbas al-Saffah. To secure his rule, Abu al-'Abbass al-Saffah sought to destroy the male line descending from Fatima and Ali,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_14_IqAbbs1] and had about 300 members of the Umayyad family killed.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_15_IqAbbs1] The last 80 Umayyads were tricked into attending a banquet with their hosts in Damascus and massacred there.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_16_IqAbbs1] (One twenty-year-old prince, Abd al-Rahman, famously managed to escape this fate: he dodged assassins all the way to Spain, where he founded an Umayyad Emirate). The First Abbasid Caliphate Period ended in 946 CE when the Daylamite Buyids from northwestern Iran reduced the caliph to a nominal figurehead. Ironically, given the bloody manner in which the dynasty began, the final Abbasid caliph was rolled up in his own carpet and trampled to death by Mongol horsemen in 1258 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_17_IqAbbs1] The zenith of the Abbasid period is considered to be the reign of Harun al Rashid (763-809 CE), whose rule is described in The Thousand and One Nights.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_18_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Population and political organization[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The capital of the Abbasid Caliphate eventually settled at Baghdad, but in the earlier years the central administration was run from Kufa (750-762 CE), Al-Raqqah (796-809 CE), Merv (810-819 CE),[MAJIDBENAM_REF_19_IqAbbs1] and Samarra (836-870 CE).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_20_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_IqAbbs1] The Abbasid caliph, spiritual leader of the Sunni Muslim world and commander-in-chief of its army, left the day-to-day administration to his vizier and heads of the diwans in the complex bureaucracy.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The departments were divided into three main areas of responsibility: the chancery (diwan-al-rasa'il); tax collection (diwan al-kharif); and army administration (diwan al-jaysh).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_IqAbbs1] Professional officials and soldiers were paid both in cash and in kind.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_IqAbbs1] The task of organizing the 'collection and payment of revenues' fell to the Abbasid military.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_IqAbbs1] However, while it was a professional institution, it lacked a rigid hierarchy or a well-defined officer class.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_IqAbbs1] Below the caliph himself, the top military rulers were the provincial governors in Iraq, Mesopotamia, Egypt, Syria, Western Iran and Khuzistan. In Iraq and Egypt, local government was divided into a hierarchy of districts, with subdivisions (kura, tassuj and rustaq) used for assessing taxation, which was passed to the governor.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_IqAbbs1] Within the Abbasid Caliphate there were also relatively independent vassals, who were required to pay tribute to the central government at Baghdad.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_27_IqAbbs1] The law code was based largely on sharia law and the ijma' (legal opinions of religious scholars).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Abbasid state provided centres of medical care, built ornate public markets, often with drinking fountains, and furnished welfare for the poor.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_IqAbbs1] As paper technology diffused from China, libraries became a common fixture in the cities of the caliphate. In Baghdad, the Khizanat al-Hikma, or 'treasury of wisdom', became a refuge for scholars, providing access to a large collection as well as free lodgings and board.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_IqAbbs1] Each important city included an official called the saheb al-sorta, who was responsible for maintaining public order, and the amir al-suq, in charge of regulating the bazaar.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The territory possessed by the caliphate was lost in dramatic fashion, shrinking from 11.1 million square kilometres in 750 CE, to 4.6 million around 850 CE, to just 1 million square kilometres half a century later as Egypt, Afghanistan and Central Asia were all lost.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_32_IqAbbs1] Nevertheless, in 900 CE the core region of Abbasid control in the Middle East still had a substantial population of about 10 million people.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_33_IqAbbs1] Over 300,000 (or maybe 900,000) of these lived in Baghdad,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_34_IqAbbs1] which by this date had probably outgrown Byzantine Constantinople.

_ Provincial line _[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Direct control took place in territories closest to the imperial centre. Iraq, Mesopotamia, Egypt, Syria, Western Iran and Khuzistan were governed directly. Other areas were largely autonomous. In some territories military governors were appointed while in others local elites paid tribute and maintained autonomy. These tributes would have been paid to one of the departments associated above. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_51_IqAbbs1] Beginning in 945 CE, the Caliphate lost substantial powers of authority and was reduced in its ability to control outlying territory. [MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Ruler of a district
"In every district of your governorship, you should appoint a trusted observer [amin] who will keep you informed of the activities of your local officials and will write to you regularly about their way of life and doings, in such a way that you will be, as it were, an eyewitness of every official's complete activities within his sphere of responsibility." Bosworth notes of 'amin': "Here obviously the equivalent of the sahib al-barid, postmaster and intelligence officer, of the Abbasid caliphate and of the later mushrif al-mamlakah of eastern Iranian states". ♠ Religious levels ♣ 2 ♥1. Caliph as head of the Sunni Muslim umma.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
2. Imams, successors of the prophet and leaders of the muslim world.
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]In theory the Caliphate and governors were the head of the Sunni faith, but in practice local religious scholars (ulama) and aesthetics (Sufis) increasingly attracted the wider populace as definers of doctrine. Unlike the Orthodox or Catholic faith, the structure of the Islamic faiths were not clearly hierarchical and all were equal before Allah. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_64_IqAbbs1]
♠ Military levels ♣ 6 ♥1. Amir al-mu' minin (official title of the Caliph)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
2. Amir (commander or governor of a province or army)
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
3. Qa-id (military officer)
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
4. Arif (leader of a militay unit of ten to fifteen soldiers)
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
5. Muquatila(Muslim soldiers paid a salary); Malwa(rank and file Turkish soldier)
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
6. Arrarun (irregular volunteers) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_65_IqAbbs1]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The above estimate is an oversimplification. The Shurta (police) and the Haras (guards) were responsible for the securing the capital and the security of the Caliph. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_66_IqAbbs1] In the earlier period the Caliphate relied on the service of Arab tribes from Arabia. As the empire expanded this system changed to a professional standing army paid for in cash. Given the fractured nature of the military structure this ranking system is not fully representative. By 833 CE, two totally separate military establishments existed under the caliphs. Shakiriya were entire tribal groups serving under their chiefs, and the second were Ghilman, slave soldiers serving in regiments. Each of these had separate hierarchies. Each of these also their own internal structures with soldiers recruited from Syria using older Byzantine ranks, whereas mercenary tribes recruited from North Africa or Central Asia served under their clan leaders. There were also differences depending of where the individuals were serving. Permanent garrisons differed from temporary soldiers used for a campaign. From 833 CE, Turkic tribesmen became increasingly integral to the military of the Caliphate. By 936 CE, the Caliph lost even the pretext of military authority. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_67_IqAbbs1]

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥
♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_68_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Men were appointed to command armies for different reasons: they were loyal to the regime, they could recruit followers and attract men to their service, they could organise the collection and payment of revenues and they were effective and knowledgeable commanders in battle. ... In the main, however, it is unhelpful to think of a hierarchy, of generals, or of an officer class."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_69_IqAbbs1]
♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_70_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The armies of the Umayyad and early 'Abbasid period were paid stipends in minted coin. At the beginning of this period, these stipends could be thought of as the hereditary right of those whose names appeared in the diwan registers. During the course of the second/eighth century, the Caliphs and their representatives forced a transformation so that the stipend became a true salary, a payment for work done. Unlike their contemporaries in the West, the soldiers of the Caliphs were never given land grants in lieu of salaries. Sometimes they might be given houses or plots of land on which to build, but not to provide an alternative income. One the rare occasions when larger land grants were given to soldiers it was as a reward for past services, rather than payment for continuing and future ones. Both Umayyads and 'Abbasids normally maintained the separation of the military from tax-collecting: apart from dire emergencies, soldiers were never given the right to collect taxes with which to pay themselves. This was always done by the government diwans."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_71_IqAbbs1]
♠ Professional priesthood ♣ absent ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_72_IqAbbs1]absent as Islam did not have a professional priesthood.

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_73_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The bureaucrats ran the various Diwans. These departments were divided into three main areas of responsibility. Officials could be found in the Chancery (diwan-al-rasa’il), the department responsible for tax collection (diwan al-kharif), and the department overseeing the army (diwan al-jaysh). Court expenses and pensions were handled by separate administrations who were also long term employees.
♠ Source of support ♣ state salary ♥Abbasid government was "based on bureaucratic methods of taxation and disbursements to the court and the army. The central government staffs collected revenues from the countryside and made payments in cash and kind to the officials and soldiers who served the state." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_74_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_75_IqAbbs1]Court expenses and pensions were handled by separate administrations who were also long term employees. The Abbasid Caliphates' ability to gain tax receipts for the imperial centre was of variable efficiency.
♠ Examination system ♣ absent ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_76_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]There was no formal system to test a scribe's suitability for the civil service.
♠ Merit promotion ♣ disputed_present_absent ♥ Expert disagreement.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]According to Van Berkel, El Cheikh, Kennedy and Osti the two most important factors for promotion were kinship and patronage.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_77_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]However, according to Lapidus the Abbasids "returned to the principles of Umar II, The Abbasids swept away Arab caste supremacy and accepted the universal equality of Muslims." Persians and Nestorian Christians were heavily represented in the bureaucracy. "Jews were active in administrative and commercial activities. Shi'i families were also prominent." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_78_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Furthermore the "Abbasid policy of recruiting notables regardless of ethnic background not only soothed the conflicts that racked the Umayyad dynasty but was essential if a centralized government was to be built at all."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_79_IqAbbs1]
♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ present ♥ e.g. The administrative offices for the court at Baghdad. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_IqAbbs1]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_81_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the Abbasid Caliphate formal the law was promulgated by a body known as the Fuqaha. The law code was heavily influenced by Sharia law. Sharia was based on the Sunna, which were teachings of the Prophet Muhammad, and the Quran, the holy book of Islam. Legal thought was also influenced by Ijma’, which were a body of rulings on legal issues based on the consensus of scholars who had met to discuss specific cases. Despite the Caliphate’s claims to religious authority based on their links to the Prophet Muhammed, it was rare for direct rulings on legal matters to originate from the caliphal authorities. Alongside a developing legal code was the development of the Qudis, who were full time judiciary officials.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_82_IqAbbs1]
♠ Judges ♣ disputed_absent_present ♥ We code present for specialist judges. If judges were "multicompetent state officials" it does not appear they are specialists who only judge law. For similar case e.g. the Roman Principate. On the other hand, another source says Qadis were "full time judiciary officials" so maybe there were some specialists.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the Abbasid Caliphate formal the law was promulgated by a body known as the Fuqaha. The law code was heavily influenced by Sharia law. Sharia was based on the Sunna, which were teachings of the Prophet Muhammad, and the Quran, the holy book of Islam. Legal thought was also influenced by Ijma’, which were a body of rulings on legal issues based on the consensus of scholars who had met to discuss specific cases. Despite the Caliphate’s claims to religious authority based on their links to the Prophet Muhammed, it was rare for direct rulings on legal matters to originate from the caliphal authorities. Alongside a developing legal code was the development of the Qudis, who were full time judiciary officials.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_83_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]At least in the Umayyad period judges were "multicompetent state officials dealing with justice, police, tax, and finance issues." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_84_IqAbbs1] Judges were appointed and were called Qadi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_85_IqAbbs1]
♠ Courts ♣ absent ♥Court proceedings took place either in a Judge's own residence, the main mosque of the city or in the palace.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_86_IqAbbs1]
♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ inferred absent ♥ no specialist court or judge so lawyer unlikely to be a specialist?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"In legal matters, Patricia Crone points out, "there is no trace of the Prophetic tradition until about 770" and it was the lawyers in particular who created the stories about Mohammed simply to back up their own arguments in law. "Numerous Prophetic traditions can be shown to have originated as statements made by the lawyers themselves ... it was the lawyers who determined what the Prophet said, not the other way around." Bukhari is said to have accumulated as many as 600,000 traditions, of which he only accepted as authentic 7,000, or just over one per cent!" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_87_IqAbbs1] -- these are religious scholars not lawyers as this variable codes? lawyers do "red tape", defend, prosecute, submit claims etc.

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥
♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥
♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ At the cities of Samarra and Baghdad.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_88_IqAbbs1] The Abbasid Caliphate marked the beginning of a large scale rebuilding of infrastructure in the Islamic tradition as former facilities appropriated from pre-Islamic peoples subsequently conquered by the Ummayads. The Round city of Baghdad contained specially built administrative offices for the court. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_89_IqAbbs1] State provided hospitals, welfare for poor, drinking fountains.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_90_IqAbbs1] "during the Sassanid era waste water systems were quite common. After the Sassanids this trend was forgotten, except in a number of urban areas such as Esfahan, a number of cities in the provinces of Guilan and Mazandaran and in Astarabad. Instead latrine wells were dug for this purpose."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_91_IqAbbs1]
♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ The foundation of new cities like Samarra led to an expansion of irrigation networks. Further evidence is provided by the large number of manuals on irrigation and land management that survive from the period. Iraq's new cities were supported by a vast network of new water works. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_92_IqAbbs1]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ present ♥ Water provision was an important aspect of Abbasid city building. In the city of Fusat, water provision was provided by the cutting of large cisterns, pack animals carrying bags of water, and in richer homes water supplies for drinking and cooking/bathing. The Abbasid Caliphate also adopted the use of qanats, a persian tecnology. There is also evidence of the use of augmented Byzantine infrastructure, with the use of lifting devices to redirect supplies. The output of a large amount of drainpipes and other containers in potter workshops indicates sanitation and water as important concerns. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_93_IqAbbs1] Water was crucial in Mosques because of the cleansing rituals. Drinking fountains were called Sabil.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_94_IqAbbs1]
♠ markets ♣ present ♥The Markets of Alexandria and Baghdad saw goods from China to Andalusia traded in purpose built areas. The Islamic Suq was built next to the central mosque of the Islamic city and represented the commercial heart of urban spaces. Mosques were integral to the commercial activities and shared the space of the market place in cities conquered by the Abassids.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_95_IqAbbs1]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present♥ During a grain crisis involving soldiers in 920 CE, the Caliph had to open the granaries [MAJIDBENAM_REF_96_IqAbbs1]. There is also archaeological evidence of depressions in the walls of residences and palaces for the purpose of food storage.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_97_IqAbbs1]
♠ Symbolic building ♣ present ♥ The Great Mosque of Samarra is an example, as well as the Mosque in general. The Mosque formed the social and architectural hub of daily life in the Islamic world.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_98_IqAbbs1]
♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ Baghdad had an estimated 150 baths. There is surviving evidence of racetracks and hunting reserves, and polo courts.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_99_IqAbbs1]
♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ present ♥ Libraries became a common fixture in the cities of the Abbasid Caliphate, in part because the tecnology of paper making had diffused from China. Ruler's completed to create and mantain vast and varied collections. In Baghdad, the Hkizanant al-hikma, or 'treasury of wisdom' became a refuge for scholars, providing access to a large collection, as well as free lodgings and board. In Mosul around 930 CE, the govenor provided free paper on which researchers could take notes on. Wealthy individuals maintained there own private collections. Buildings themselves were furnished with bookshelves, catalogues and a trained staff. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_100_IqAbbs1]
♠ Roads ♣ present ♥ The vast network of routes to facilitate the religious journey of the Hajj best exemplify the types of road networks maintained by the Abbasid Caliphate. Whereas the previous Hajj route had originated in Damascus, the rising importance of Baghdad saw a development of guard post, paved roads and watering stations across the deserts between that city and Mecca.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_101_IqAbbs1] [MAJIDBENAM_REF_102_IqAbbs1]
♠ Bridges ♣ present ♥ The bridges of Samarra are an example of Bridge making during the Abassid Caliphate. Its inscriptions and brickwork stand out. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_103_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_REF_104_IqAbbs1]
♠ Canals ♣ present ♥ Canals were a vital component to the core cities constructed during the Abasid Caliphate. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_105_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_REF_106_IqAbbs1]
♠ Ports ♣ present ♥ Baghdad was near large rivers that connected it to wider trade networks. Ports along the Persian gulf also provided outlets to trade.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_107_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_REF_108_IqAbbs1]

♠ extent ♣ 245 ♥ Great Mosque of Samarra. Building took 3 years, 848-851 CE. Height of tower 52 meters. Width of tower 33 meters. Based on satellite photograph and distance measurement calculator on Google maps the walled perimeter of the destroyed Mosque measured 245m*160m.

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ present ♥ The Ilm al-Miqat astrological literature led to the creation of vast tables, ranging from simple tables compiled in Baghdad in the ninth century CE to measure solar or stellar altitudes effect on prayer time to more sophisticated tables dictating what tens of thousands of entries for finding the time of day or night by the sun or any star on multiple latitudes. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_116_IqAbbs1]
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ Islamic prayer times are astronomically defined, as is determining the direction of Mecca. As such, astrological timekeeping, the body of literature called 'ilm-al-miquat' replaced folk astronomy with mathematical precision.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_117_IqAbbs1]
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ present ♥ The Quran
♠ Religious literature ♣ present ♥ The closest proximity to theology is the Arabic Kalam. The complexities of this are beyond a single entry, but Islamic writers wrote synopses of Islamic theological thought in volumes called Aqidat. Abu Hanifah's testiment is one of the best known, being the work of Abu Ja'far Ahmad al-Tawawi dating from 933 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_118_IqAbbs1] Muhammad al-Bukhari (810-870 CE): "Bukhara-born compiler and editor of An Abridged Collection of Authentic Hadiths with Connected Chains [of Transmission] Regarding Matters Pertaining to the Prophet, His Practices, and His Times, the most revered book in Islam after the Quran."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_119_IqAbbs1]
♠ Practical literature ♣ present ♥ Examples include Descriptive and practical manuals, didactic treaties for the training and guidance of secretaries. Furthermore, biographical materials and collections of anecdotes on viziers and secretaries were also produced. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_120_IqAbbs1]
♠ History ♣ present ♥ Abu-Ja'far Muhammed (c. 923 CE) wrote the definitive historical work of the period, the Ta'risk al-rusul wa-l-muluk, which was held in the highest esteem for the next three centuries. He and other writers had access to vast records and correspondences of the state, allowing for a large degree of evidence and checking of sources.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_121_IqAbbs1] "this was the greatest period of growth for the Arabic language and literature, as well as for the development of Islamic histories based on the romance of Bedouin lore."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_122_IqAbbs1]
♠ Philosophy ♣ present ♥ Examples abound, one such being Al-Farabi's Al-jam' bayna ra'yay al-hakimayn aflatun al-ilahi-wa arostitalis, translated as 'Harmonizations of the Opinions of Plato and Aristotle'. Another tradition was on the relationship of the philosopher and the city, such as Ibn Sina's visionary recitals, the Hayy b. Yaqzan. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_IqAbbs1] (Governor?) Tahir wrote an advice for rulers for his son, an epistle, which became famous and is copied out in full by al-Tabari in Volume XXXII pages 110-128.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_IqAbbs1]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ present ♥ Works on Physics, Mathematics and Applied Science, such as Mechanical Technology survive from the period. One of the outstanding examples is the Kitab al-Hiyal (Book of ingenious devices), detailing over 100 devices ranging from self filling lamps and fountains to a primitive gas mask. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_125_IqAbbs1] "The brothers Jafar, Ahmad, and Hasan ibn Musa from Merv, known as the “Sons of Musa” (Banu Musa). In ninth-century Baghdad they dominated the scientific scene under Caliph Mamun and his successors. Besides their work in geometry and astronomy, Ahmad wrote a pioneering work in practical mechanics, Book of Ingenious Devices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_IqAbbs1] Ahmad al-Farghani (c.797-860 CE). "An astronomer who hailed from the Ferghana Valley in present-day Uzbekistan. Farghani’s The Elements was among the earliest works on astronomy to be written in Arabic."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_IqAbbs1]
♠ Fiction ♣ present ♥ Abbasid writers produced a large number of Belles-Lettres during the period. Topics ranged from fables and legends to poetry on topics as wide ranging as hunting poems called the tardiyyat to politics. Also, they Abbasids oversaw the translation of fiction works from earlier Greek and Persian sources. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_128_IqAbbs1] "this was the greatest period of growth for the Arabic language and literature, as well as for the development of Islamic histories based on the romance of Bedouin lore."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_129_IqAbbs1]

♠ Indigenous coins ♣ present ♥ The gold Dinar, the silver dirham, copper daniq. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_131_IqAbbs1] There were two principle coins in circulation, the gold Dinar and the silver dirham. This was in part a legacy of the conquest of Byzantine and Sassanian territories where the two coins were the major form of currency. The ratio of exchange was twenty dirhams to the dinar. From approximately 800 CE-950 CE a copper coin called the daniqs seems to have been in circulation. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_132_IqAbbs1] By 819 CE the coinage was increasingly being debased. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_133_IqAbbs1]
♠ Paper currency ♣ absent ♥
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ present ♥ With the tax collectors came "bankers and money changers to convert currencies or to give credits." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_134_IqAbbs1] "A burgeoning class of merchants, traders and bankers ran rings around the theoretical Islamic strictures against usury, for example. Maxime Rodinson cites the example of books that circulated as early as the ninth century that dealt specifically with ruses (hiyal in Arabic) to get around the theological limitations of trading. One of the many simple ways of getting around the ban on interest, for example, goes something like this: Imagine Yusuf wants to borrow 100 dirhams from Ahmed. Ahmed sells Yusuf a book, for, say, 120 dirhams, payable in a year's time. Yusuf then sells the book back to Ahmed immediately with cash, for 100 dirhams. Thus Ahmed still has his book, Yusuf trousers 100 dirhams but he has an obligation to pay for the book he 'bought' for 120 dirhams ... twelve months later."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_135_IqAbbs1]

♠ Couriers ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_136_IqAbbs1] Royal couriers carried messages and directives of the court. a 'hamami' was a "despatcher of carrier pigeons and letters from one town to another" in Iraq, Egypt and Syria: 9th, 10th 11th CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_137_IqAbbs1]
♠ Postal stations ♣ present ♥ Simple postal stations in use as stopping point for couriers. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_138_IqAbbs1] The Abbasid had a department of state running the post office, called the Barim. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_139_IqAbbs1] "The Muslim conquerors adop­ted many ancient institutions, including the postal system, which they called barīd (ultimately derived from Lat. veredus, Gk. beredos “[courier’s] horse”). Although there is some controversy over whether it was primarily the Byzantine or Sasanian model that was followed (see, e.g. EI2, s.v. Barīd; Camb. Hist. Iran III/1, p. 564), it is probable that elements of both were taken over (Mez, p. 466). In the eastern part of the empire at least, ancient Persian practices and terminology seem to have prevailed."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_140_IqAbbs1]
♠ General postal service ♣ inferred present ♥ "Only rarely were private individuals allowed to make use of the post (Bayhaqī, ed. Schwally, p. 429)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_141_IqAbbs1] a 'hamami' was a "despatcher of carrier pigeons and letters from one town to another" in Iraq, Egypt and Syria: 9th, 10th 11th CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_142_IqAbbs1]

Compound bows and Crossbows were present, as well as more esoteric weaponry such as fire arrows, were used on some occasions. Volunteers and informal levies were reported to have used slings, makeshift spears and other unconventional weapons. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_152_IqAbbs1]

This unique painting is contemporary with Tang China, displaying how the traction trebuchet was used along the Silk Road."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_154_IqAbbs1]

♠ Incendiaries ♣ present ♥ Greek fire: "The Arabs, however, soon learned the exact composition of the combustible mixture and used it themselves in Asia, where they transmitted it to the Chinese. Indeed, by the year 850 even crew members of Arab trading vessels in the Indian Ocean would use it to protect their ships against pirates, and during the reign of the Abbasid Caliph al-Mutawakkil each corps of archers in the infantry had a special division of naphtha-throwers called naffatun, who wore fireproof suits, perhaps of asbestos, and hurled "grenades" at the enemy by hand or with catapults. Some of the "grenades" were astoundingly similar in size and shape to those used in modern times: round iron - or clay - jars which contained the flammable mixture and exploded shortly after the lids were opened."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_158_IqAbbs1] Persians threw vessels filled with sulphur, asphalt and naphtha into the enemy bulwarks. The first mention of the common place use of naphtha as an incendiary device in the annals of Islam is in connection with a raid in India in 777. During the siege of Heraklia in 803, however, stone balls wrapped in linen and covered with burning naphtha were hurled by mangonels against the ramparts. The fire would cling to the stones and cause them to burst. From that time onwards, the caliphate employed corps of naphtha-throwers and the Muslim fleets were provided with it as well. In order to protect themselves, people covered flammable materials with newly flayed animal skins and those who handled the naphtha wore clothes saturated with talc. Naphtha-filled glass grenades appeared first in 934, unless in that year the weapons were actually incendiary javelins, but the use of fire grenades was firmly established a few years later. ... The latest historian of Greek fire, Maurice Mercier, admits that during the thirteenth century, people began to use grenades filled with mealed gunpowder, called 'Syrian jugs', which had a most explosive force.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_159_IqAbbs1]

♠ Specialized military vessels ♣ present ♥ The Abbasid Caliphate was not a naval power in the Mediterranean. The Umayyad Caliphate had faced substantial losses at sea with Greek crewed ships, and the Abbasid never attempted to blockade Constantinople from the sea. Furthermore, while the Caliphs controlled the coastlines and had freedom of movement along this territory, it lacked both the facilities to build military ships and the raw materials to facilitate this endeavor. The situation in the Persian gulf was different, as large trade fleets plied the waters between Iraq and India, and down the Horn of Africa. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_184_IqAbbs1] Territorial losses outside of the core territories in Egypt and Syria further weakened the capacity of the Abassid Caliphs capacity to wage naval warfare. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_185_IqAbbs1]

♠ Ditch ♣ present ♥ Ditches were used in this region in the Middle Ages. No specific reference.
♠ Moat ♣ inferred present ♥ Abbasid siege of Al-Wasit, last Umayyad stronghold in Iraq: "In the first such encounter Umayyad forces were defeated, and they retreated to the moat that surrounded the western section of the city."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_187_IqAbbs1]
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ present ♥ As used around Baghdad. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_188_IqAbbs1] The technology to create fortifications was present, but in the case of large cities not implemented as the Caliphs preferred battles over sieges, and because of concerns that citizens would use them for protection during revolts. The Arabic word for castle or fortress was Hisn, with the Qasr more often used for a fortress. The use of fortifications depended on local tradition. In Syria, pre-existing walls were maintained. In other areas of conquest or after rebellions fortifications were torn down. Baghdad stands out as an exception in terms of a fortified urban centre. Baghdad was surrounded by large walls, and fortified gates were secured with two sets of iron covered doors and large numbers of guards. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_189_IqAbbs1]
♠ Fortified camps ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Complex fortifications ♣ suspected unknown ♥ As with the walls and gates around Baghdad. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_190_IqAbbs1] -- more than one ring of walls?

♠ Modern fortifications ♣ absent ♥Greek fire/naft was used from at least 751 CE, and crude petroleum was used for incendiary purposes.

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present: 750-946 CE; absent: 946-1258 CE ♥after 946 CE the Daylamite Buyids took over civil and military power, while the Abbasid Caliphs endured as figureheads of Islam.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_192_IqAbbs1]
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ absent ♥ "Muslim armies were paid in cash and were expected to purchase their supplies (at least when they were in Muslim territory)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_193_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Equipment varied by the place of origin of the military units. Steppe tribes came equipped with their own mounts, weaponry and equipment, whereas the Syrian infantry used uniform equipment mimicking earlier Byzantium precedence. The Ghilman, as property of the state, were by necessity equipped and maintained by the Caliphate. Independent states nominally under the control of Caliphate varied considerably in terms of equipment and method of organisation, especially in the Steppe and North Africa. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_194_IqAbbs1]

♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥
♠ Sources of recruits ♣ domestic; foreign; mercenary ♥The Abbasid Caliphate recruited from its own population, as well as purchasing and training non-muslim slave soldiers known as the Ghilman, later referred to as the Mamelukes. The Caliphate also recruited entire ethnic groups from the frontiers to serve the Caliphate. One of the innovations of the Caliphate was to recruit outside of the original Arab tribes for both administrative and military purposes. Permanent military garrisons were placed in Syria facing the Byzantine frontier, and outpost placed in other areas deemed at risk. The Shakiriya were calvary units recruited from tribal peoples in North Africa and the Steppe, whilst the second and increasingly important force were the Ghilman, non-muslim slave soldiers serving the Caliphate and counterbalancing the other forces. Turkic forces increasingly monopolized the chance to serve in the armed forces from Arabs. Haras (guards) were responsible for the securing the capitol and the security of the Caliph and were also a permanent force recruited locally. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_202_IqAbbs1]
♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by soldiers; by state ♥ Resources were acquired by soldiers but paid for by the state. The diwan al-jaysh was the department tasked with handling all military expenses. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_203_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Muslim armies were paid in cash and were expected to purchase their supplies (at least when they were in Muslim territory)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_204_IqAbbs1]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ Battle Of Zab 750 CE: 120,000 (Umayyad) vs. between 12,000-20,000 (Abbasid). The battle resulted in the collapse of the Umayyad Caliphate and the ascension of the Abbasid. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_205_IqAbbs1]
♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ Caliph Mamun’s rationalist inquisition 833-848 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_206_IqAbbs1] Abbasid vs Umayyad.
♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ Caliph Mamun’s rationalist inquisition 833-848 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_207_IqAbbs1] Abbasid vs Umayyad.
♠ Popular uprisings ♣ present ♥ Iranian/Shi'i rebellions.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_208_IqAbbs1]
♠ Military revolts ♣ present ♥ Present.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_209_IqAbbs1]
♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ present ♥ Tahir ibn Husayn, governor of Khurasan, in 822 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_210_IqAbbs1] Qarmatian rebellion. Tulunid war. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_211_IqAbbs1] Iranian/Shi'i rebellions. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_212_IqAbbs1] Central Asian rebellions: "rebellion was mounted in Khurasan by one Sinbad (or Sunpad), a Zoroastrian who raised an army of 100,000 on the proposition that Abu Muslim had not died but had turned into a white dove. Another Central Asian rebel was called Ishak, 'The Turk,' an illiterate follower of Abu Muslim who declared himself the successor of Zoroaster. Then an ethnic Persian cloth worker from Merv named Hashim, son of Hakim, who had risen to general rank under Abu Muslim, declared himself the successor to every prophet from Moses and Christ through Muhammad and, significantly, Abu Muslim himself. Wearing a green cloth over his presumably disfigured face, al-Muqanna, or 'The Veiled One,' rallied the populace in the region from Bukhara through present-day Tajikistan to avenge the Abbasids in the name of his one true faith."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_213_IqAbbs1]
♠ rural succession ♣ present ♥ "In the eastern provinces [the Caliph's] authority was on the whole more fully recognized, with the exception of the relatively limited territory remaining in the hands of Babak, a rebel who, allied to the sect of the Khurramiyya, revolted in Azarbayjan in 201 /816, and from his mountain retreats resisted all the expeditions sent against him by the central power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_214_IqAbbs1]
♠ Violent civil conflict ♣ present ♥ E.g. against Shia groups. "Meanwhile al-Mansur was obliged, from the very beginning of his reign, to take action against the Shi'is. [...] The revolt at the same period of two claimants, both Shi'i descendants of al-Hasan b. 'All, was more serious. The first, Muhammad, called al-Nafs al-Zakiyja,' the Pure Soul', a great-grandson of al-Hasan, had not given his oath to al-Saffah and had remained in hiding since the accession of the 'Abbasids. [...] Muhammad was [...] induced to come out in open rebellion at Medina and to denounce al-Mansur as a tyrant who did not respect Islamic law. Notwithstanding the support he received from certain religious scholars, he was unable to rally a sufficiently large number of adherents and was quickly crushed by the caliph's troops in Ramadan 145/December 762, when his brother Ibrahim had also just revolted in the region of Basra. For a time Ibrahim constituted a serious threat to the towns of 'Iraq, but he too was soon defeated by al-Mansur's general, his cousin 'Isa b. Miisa. The suppression of these two 'And revolts, although securing several years of peace for the caliph, set him definitely among the enemies of the family of the Prophet."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_215_IqAbbs1]

Thus, for instance, al-Kazim spent most of his time in the Abbasid prison in Baghdad, where he was poisoned to death in 800. Similarly, Hasan al-Askari was imprisoned for some time and then kept under surveillance in Samarra, then the Abbasid capital, where he died in 873-74." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_232_IqAbbs1]

[MAJIDBENAM_REF_234_IqAbbs1] "The Carmathian leader, the sahib al-khal, after being seized and made a prisoner, was tortured in Baghdad as a public spectacle."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_235_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]“the language and penalties of the ḥirāba verse were co-opted and imitated in historical practice…Mu'awiya is said to have ordered that one of alHujr's supporters be burned alive. The heads of al-Husayn and his supporters were severed and paraded. In the same year there were summary executions of several Khawarjj, and in one case the same year [680 CE], a Khariji was tortured with the sanctions identified in the ḥirāba verse; his feet and hands were amputated and then he was crucified. Eventually it became common practice for the Umayyads and early Abbasids to execute rebels and mutilate their bodies."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_236_IqAbbs1]. As Biruni wrote, the Arab general Qutayba 'extinguished and ruined in every possible way all those who knew how to write and read the Khwarazmian writing, who knew the history of the country, and who studied the sciences.'"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_245_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Archaeological proxies:

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Hajj ♥ "In the pilgrimage to Mecca, the hajj, ancient Arab rites have been taken over and spiritualized. The pilgrimage is performed during the last lunar month, Dhu'l­hijja. People who come to Arabia at other times can perform the lesser pilgrimage, umra, which consists mainly of the circumambulation of the Ka'ba and the running between two hillocks called Safa and Marwa. Pilgrimage to a sacred place requires a ritual consecration. Thus, at a certain distance from Mecca the men, with shaven heads, put on a special garment, ihram, consisting of two white unsewn pieces of cloth that cover their whole body; women put on a covering garment. Pilgrims greet the sacred precincts with the call labbaika, "Here I am at Thy service!" The rites that have to be performed include the sevenfold circumambulation of the Ka'ba (where one tries to kiss the black stone in its southeastern corner) and a sevenfold running between Safa and Marwa, which are now connected by covered arcades. But the central rite is the "staying" on the hill 'Arafat*, a distance of some eight miles from Mecca, on the ninth day of the month. There, sermons are given, and on the way back the 'stoning of Satan' is performed by casting seven pebbles three times at a certain place. After that, on the tenth day of the month, each pilgrim slaughters a sheep or larger animal in Mina. Those staying home do the same. This part of the ritual commemorates the day when Abraham was ordered to sacrifice his son Isma'il, whose place was then taken by a ram. After the Feast of Offering the pilgrim can return from the sacred state, ihram, into the normal state, when the cutting of hair and nails, as well as sex, are again permitted. Killing any creatures inside the sacred area is considered a major sin. The pilgrimage should be performed only when the pilgrim is in good health and can undertake the journey without major difficulties, especially without incurring debts; this shows the realistic approach of Islam to religious practice. In former centuries the pilgrimage was a dangerous undertaking for those who came from distant lands, whether they traveled overland in the caravans which started, with great pomp, in Cairo or Baghdad, or sailed from India or Malaysia on fragile boats. (Air travel is not always very safe either.) Yet modern Muslims sometimes express the view that these slow, arduous journeys prepared them better for the great event than the brief trip by air, which barely leaves leisure to meditate on the experience of visiting the center of their religion. On their return the pilgrims bring a vessel filled with wholesome water from the well Zamzam, from which Hagar once drank. They are received by their families with pride and joy, and are called Hajji. Those who die during the pilgrimage are regarded as martyrs, shahid, "witness" for their faith. Doubtlessly the meeting of people from all parts of the world in Mecca and their common worship at the central sanctuary of Islam does much to strengthen the feeling of unity among Muslims. Through the centuries many scholars and pious people extended their stay in Mecca and exchanged their religious views with colleagues from other parts of the world. The sojourn near the Ka'ba inspired important theological and mystical works: the comprehensive commentary on the Koran by Zamakhshari (called by the honorific title Jar Allah, "God's neighbor" due to his prolonged stay in Mecca) grew out of the author's stay in Mecca. Even more evident is the influence of the Meccan experience on the most comprehensive work of Islamic theosophy, the Futuhat al­makkiyya, of Ibn 'Arabi, which was "revealed" to the author while he was circumambulating the Ka'ba. Furthermore, it is important to remember that almost all reform movements in the fringe areas of the Islamic world were triggered off by the pilgrimage. Faithful believers from Bengal and Nigeria, from Morocco and Central Asia, who had lived for some time in Mecca and exchanged their views with others who, like them, had experienced what seemed to be the 'true Arabic Islam', returned to their Page 39countries to fight the numerous indigenous superstitions and customs which had infiltrated the Islam of the masses and which now appeared to them like remnants of ancient paganism."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_246_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, although "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_247_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_248_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ The inhabitants of Mecca, Medina and other important waypoints along the pilgrimage may be considered as "passive spectators or witnesses", as the Seshat codebook puts it. They would witness the Hajj, or parts of it, between the tenth and the twelfth months of the Islamic calendar, every year.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_249_IqAbbs1]
♠ Duration ♣ 144 ♥ hours. 6 days From the 8th to the 13th day of the last month of the Islamic calendar..[MAJIDBENAM_REF_250_IqAbbs1] The main acts of the Hajj take place in five days of the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, that is, from the 8th to the 13th of the month of Dhu'l Hijja: however, "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_251_IqAbbs1]

♠ Orthodoxy checks ♣ present ♥ Presence of Islamic clergy.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_277_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Costs of participation
♠ currency ♣ present ♥ Although there was no official cost of participation, in that no money was charged for pilgrimage, to undertake hajj was an expensive occurrence which prohibited many from undertaking it. The pilgrim would need to travel to Mecca, would need food and accommodation for that time, would need money to buy animals for sacrifice..[MAJIDBENAM_REF_278_IqAbbs1]
♠ property/valuable items ♣ present ♥ Though this is not prescribed by sacred texts, there is a long historical tradition of the wealthiest pilgrims distributing money and precious objects (e.g. textiles) to the less wealthy.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_279_IqAbbs1]
♠ health costs ♣ present ♥ Hajj was physically demanding including a seven times repeated run between two hills and several miles trekking into the desert..[MAJIDBENAM_REF_280_IqAbbs1]
♠ animal sacrifice ♣ present ♥ On the third of the five main days of the Hajj, animal sacrifice is performed.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_281_IqAbbs1]
♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_282_IqAbbs1]
♠ human sacrifice of an in-group member ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_283_IqAbbs1]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Friday prayer ♥ "The Friday prayer is a duty of the community; it contains a short sermon, khutba*, which consists of two parts. It gives some advice usually based on the Koran or the hadith, and a prayer for the ruler of the government. To be mentioned in the khutba meant to be the legitimate ruler. Women rarely attend the Friday prayer but prefer to pray at home.The daily prayers are explained in the Koran as acts of humility and adoration. At the end of the prayer the Muslim can mention his/her personal requests. While traveling or in war or under duress, one can perform the noon and afternoon or sunset and night prayers together. One also has numerous opportunities to perform additional prayers of two or more rak'a each, e.g., during solar or lunar eclipses; when performing the rain prayer; when entering or leaving the house or the mosque—in short, some pious Muslims use every opportunity to perform a brief salat. The belief in the purifying power of ritual prayer is intense; the Prophet compared it to a stream of water that washes off sins five times a day. The performance of the prayer at the prescribed time constitutes ideally a means of educating Muslims to punctuality, cleanliness, and, since there is no ranking in the mosque, equal participation in the life of the community. Prayer can also lead to ecstatic experiences, and when one observes a praying Muslim who is oblivious of everything around him and seems to have drawn himself, as it were, out of this world to stand humbly before the Lord, one realizes best whence Islam derives its vital strength.To perform the salat, a clean spot suffices, be it in the fields, in the train, or in one's shop or office. A small prayer rug guarantees the cleanliness of the place. However, even in the Prophet's lifetime a place for congregational worship was established, called masjid, "the place where one prostrates" (hence the word Mosque). The great mosque, where the Friday prayer is held and which was found in each quarter, is called jami'*, "the gathering [mosque]."Mainly in the eastern part of the Islamic world, one finds small open prayer places called musalla*, and for the gathering of large congregations at the two 'id*, the end of Ramadan and the Feast of Offerings, one sometimes uses an 'idgah*, a vast enclosure with a prayer niche."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_285_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_286_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_287_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_288_IqAbbs1]
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Noon prayer on Fridays. Expert input needed for exact duration. at this time wiki scraper not able to read decimal, so cannot code less than 1 hour e.g. 0.05 for 15 minutes.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priesthood. Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ All Muslims, though women would frequently pray in private instead.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_289_IqAbbs1]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ "Under the Fatimids, Nawruz was expressed in an official celebration in which gifts were bestowed on officials and their families. It appears, however, that a carnival increasingly became its main function. The people drank wine and beer; there were those who ambushed travelers and sprayed them with filthy water or wine, and others threw eggs on one another. Even emirs and dignitaries were exposed to this sort of ridicule, and, to rescue themselves, they had to pay 'ransom.' Slapping one another with boots or leather mats in public places was possibly a remnant of a pagan ritual. Sexual overtones were expressed in the play of water games, causing men and women to become wet so that naked bodies could be seen through clothes. In 1188, the public gathering of transvestites and prostitutes is reported. Masquerades are reported during the celebration of 975. Puritan scholars of the Mamluk period lamented the adverse effect of the festival on both the common people and the learned. They reported that schools were shut down and turned into playgrounds and that teachers were insulted and sprayed with water. These writers found it detestable that the participants were able 'to commit all kinds of evil,' that 'there was no interdiction [to any sort of behavior] and no authority imposed.' "The high mark of Nawruz in medieval Cairo was the procession of the Emir of Nawruz, usually a wanton who was either naked or dressed in colorful clothes, his face besmeared with lime or flour and a beard of fur attached to his face, on his head a special cap made of palm leaves, riding a donkey in the streets. He held a sort of register in his hand, 'visited' homes of dignitaries to collect 'debts,' and punished those who refused to pay. Manifest in this procession is the carnivalesque element of status reversal and riotous revelry a ́la medieval Christian festivals such as the King of Fools." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_359_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "Be that as it may, in Egypt, unlike in the eastern provinces, although it did mark the beginning of the year, Nawruz was an autumn festival that was celebrated on the first day of the Coptic month of Tut (September), when the Nile was expected to reach its highest level." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_360_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "Be that as it may, in Egypt, unlike in the eastern provinces, although it did mark the beginning of the year, Nawruz was an autumn festival that was celebrated on the first day of the Coptic month of Tut (September), when the Nile was expected to reach its highest level." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_361_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "Be that as it may, in Egypt, unlike in the eastern provinces, although it did mark the beginning of the year, Nawruz was an autumn festival that was celebrated on the first day of the Coptic month of Tut (September), when the Nile was expected to reach its highest level." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_362_IqAbbs1]. (KB:Coded as 'not applicable' due to there being no evidence given for 'passive spectators')

Ḵodādād, I, p. 49; for testimonies of poets see Borumand-e Saʿid, pp. 253-384)." ♠ other ♣ present ♥ "It was precisely because Nowruz was associated from the outset with cultural memories of the splendor and divinely bestowed power of the royal courts of pre-Islamic Persia that it was attractive to rulers, from the Abbasid caliphs to the Pahlavis. Along with its many ceremonies, and most notably that of gift exchange, it provided the rulers with an alternative source of affirming and enhancing their power and prestige through a strictly non-Islamic channel; for unlike religious festivals, they could appear and be celebrated as the focal point and the peerless heroes of the occasion." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_390_IqAbbs1]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Arafat wuquf ♥ The key ritual within the wider sequence of rituals that makes up the Hajj. “On the morning of the ninth, [pilgrims] proceed towards ‘Arafat, a plain 14.5km (nine miles) from Mina, where the central rite of the Hajj, wuquf (standing) on ‘Arafat, takes place between noon and sunset, during which time many heartfelt prayers are offered. The scene reminds Muslims of the gathering on the Day of Judgement. If any pilgrim misses this event, his/her Hajj is not valid and has to be done again another year. This does not apply to any other rite, all of which can be done over a longer period or compensated for with an offering. This is the time for the pilgrims to read the Qur’an, glorify God and pray most earnestly for forgiveness and everything else they wish for. Particularly recommended for glorification and repetition is this prayer, ‘There is no God but Allah alone, with no partner. Dominion and praise belong to Him, who gives life and death. Goodness is in His hands and He has power over everything.’ This just gives a succinct summary of the Islamic faith and connects the individual pilgrim to what Ibrahim and Muhammad brought and uttered. Pilgrims normally face towards the Ka‘ba while reciting these prayers, as they do for the five daily prayers, believing that, by doing this, their prayers are more likely to be accepted.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_400_IqAbbs1]. This ritual is coded here as dysphoric due to the fact that pilgrims are required to stand for several hours (between noon and sunset) under the sun’s heat. A contemporary account, dating to 1997, notes that the “heat is virtually unbearable” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_401_IqAbbs1]. However, it is worth noting that, due to peculiarities of the Islamic calendar, this ritual may sometimes be performed at a time of the year when the temperature is less hot than at other times, and when there are fewer others between noon and sunset than at other times. Moreover, a few informal online sources suggest that those who cannot stand in the sun for the duration of the ritual are allowed to be in the shade and/or sit down. Still, though relatively mild, this appears to be the most dysphoric ritual within Sunni Islam.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar [MAJIDBENAM_REF_402_IqAbbs1].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_403_IqAbbs1]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Duration ♣ 6 ♥ hours, on average. The ritual takes place between noon and sunset.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ all Muslims[MAJIDBENAM_REF_404_IqAbbs1]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥Dysphoric elements
♠ fasting ♣ inferred present ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_405_IqAbbs1], but, because participants must devote all their energies in prayer, it seems likely that they were not allowed to eat. Then again, this may not count as fasting due to the relative brevity of the ritual (between 5 and 7 hours, depending on the time of the year when it is performed on any given year).
♠ vigil/sleep deprivation ♣ absent ♥ Though the ritual is a vigil, it takes place during the daytime [MAJIDBENAM_REF_406_IqAbbs1].
♠ fear ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_407_IqAbbs1].
♠ humiliation ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_408_IqAbbs1].
♠ disgust ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_409_IqAbbs1].
♠ poisoning/dysphoria-causing drugs ♣ absent ♥ Islam prohibits the consumption of mind-altering substances.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_410_IqAbbs1]
♠ pain ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_411_IqAbbs1].
♠ mutilation ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_412_IqAbbs1].
♠ risk of death ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_413_IqAbbs1].
♠ other ♣ present ♥ Ideally, participants would stand under the sun for several hours between noon and sunset [MAJIDBENAM_REF_414_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_REF_415_IqAbbs1].

♠ animal sacrifice ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [MAJIDBENAM_REF_432_IqAbbs1].
♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ absent ♥ [Text from sacrifice codings by Agathe] No source could be found that explicitly states Islam’s condemnation of human sacrifice. However, the fact that no source could be found that even ‘’mentions’’ human sacrifice in connection with Islam (beyond the occasional lurid, and dubious, tabloid headline), combined with the fact that a lot of information is available on the beliefs and practices underlining this religion, strongly suggests that human sacrifice is not part of the Islamic ritual repertoire. ““Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham. We are accustomed to the story, which remains a text of “terror and trembling,” as Kierkegaard put it. Abraham is commanded by god to “sacrifice” his only son Isaac, and he is willing to do so (Gen. 22). The Hebrew term for this sacrifice is colah, “make to rise upwards,” as fire and smoke rise to heaven;10 the action is to cut the throat with a knife and to burn the corpse on wood—the knife and wood expressly mentioned in the text. The human victim must be fettered in order to receive the deadly cut. Abraham does obey his god’s command, without even an attempt at protest. The text enjoys the paradox: “the son that you love”; “and the two walked together” (Gen. 22.1-19: 2;. In Muslim tradition, the place where Abraham “bound” his son is the rock on the temple hill of Jerusalem, which, since Khalif Omar, is covered by the “temple dome” with its golden cupola. Of course, an angel stops Abraham’s action at the last moment, pointing to an animal for sacrifice. The text says God was “testing” (nissah) Abraham, and his submissive obedience is highly rewarded. The text also states that god accepted the ram “instead of” (tachath) Isaac. One way to discard the terror of the story is to introduce a perspective of historical progress: God was replacing human sacrifice by animal sacrifice. This could be termed an advance in civilization. The Islamic interpretation comes close to this interpretation, as it makes this a founding myth for el eid.”
[MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_433_IqAbbs1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]

However, the fact that no source could be found that even ‘’mentions’’ human sacrifice in connection with Islam (beyond the occasional lurid, and dubious, tabloid headline), combined with the fact that a lot of information is available on the beliefs and practices underlining this religion, strongly suggests that human sacrifice is not part of the Islamic ritual repertoire. ““Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham.
